<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Frida Kahlo</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mexican painter (1907-1954)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Kahlo" redirects here. For the surname, see <a href="/wiki/Kahlo_(surname)" title="Kahlo (surname)">Kahlo (surname)</a>.</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">In this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_name" title="Spanish name">Spanish name</a>, the first or paternal <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">surname</a> is <i> Kahlo</i> and the second or maternal family name is <i> Calderón</i>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output <div style="line-height:0;margin-bottom:-2px;">​</div> <div style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:1px;">​</div>(<abbr title="married">m.</abbr> 1929; <abbr title="divorced">div.</abbr> 1939)<wbr/>​</div><br/><div style="line-height:0;margin-bottom:-3px;">​</div>
<div style="display:inline;white-space:nowrap;"> <div style="display:inline-block;">​</div>(<abbr title="married">m.</abbr> 1940)<wbr/>​</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Parents</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Kahlo" title="Guillermo Kahlo">Guillermo Kahlo</a> (father)</li><li>Matilde Calderón y González (mother)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Relatives</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Cristina_Kahlo" title="Cristina Kahlo">Cristina Kahlo</a> (sister)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Magdalena Carmen Frida Kahlo y Calderón</b> (<small>Spanish pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="es-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">[ˈfɾiða ˈkalo]</a></span>; 6 July 1907 – 13 July 1954<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup>) was a Mexican painter known for her many portraits, <a href="/wiki/Self-portrait" title="Self-portrait">self-portraits</a>, and works inspired by the nature and artifacts of Mexico. Inspired by <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Mexico" title="Culture of Mexico">the country's popular culture</a>, she employed a <a href="/wiki/Na%C3%AFve_art" title="Naïve art">naïve</a> <a href="/wiki/Folk_art" title="Folk art">folk art</a> style to explore questions of identity, <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">postcolonialism</a>, gender, class, and race in Mexican society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Her paintings often had strong autobiographical elements and mixed realism with fantasy. In addition to belonging to the post-revolutionary <i><a href="/wiki/Mexicayotl" title="Mexicayotl">Mexicayotl</a></i> movement, which sought to define a Mexican identity, Kahlo has been described as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surrealist" title="Surrealist">surrealist</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magical_realist" title="Magical realist">magical realist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> She is known for painting about her experience of <a href="/wiki/Chronic_pain" title="Chronic pain">chronic pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordPain_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordPain-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Born to a German father and a <i><a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">mestiza</a></i> mother, Kahlo spent most of her childhood and adult life at La Casa Azul, her family home in <a href="/wiki/Coyoac%C3%A1n" title="Coyoacán">Coyoacán</a> – now publicly accessible as the <a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo_Museum" title="Frida Kahlo Museum">Frida Kahlo Museum</a>. Although she was disabled by <a href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a> as a child, Kahlo had been a promising student headed for medical school until being injured in a bus accident at the age of 18, which caused her lifelong pain and medical problems. During her recovery, she returned to her childhood interest in art with the idea of becoming an artist.
</p><p>Kahlo's interests in politics and art led her to join the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Communist_Party" title="Mexican Communist Party">Mexican Communist Party</a> in 1927,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> through which she met fellow Mexican artist <a href="/wiki/Diego_Rivera" title="Diego Rivera">Diego Rivera</a>. The couple married in 1929<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and spent the late 1920s and early 1930s travelling in Mexico and the United States together. During this time, she developed her artistic style, drawing her main inspiration from <a href="/wiki/Mexican_handcrafts_and_folk_art" title="Mexican handcrafts and folk art">Mexican folk culture</a>, and painted mostly small self-portraits that mixed elements from <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_Mexico" title="Pre-Columbian Mexico">pre-Columbian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> beliefs. Her paintings raised the interest of Surrealist artist <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Breton" title="André Breton">André Breton</a>, who arranged for Kahlo's first solo exhibition at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julien_Levy_Gallery" title="Julien Levy Gallery">Julien Levy Gallery</a> in New York in 1938; the exhibition was a success and was followed by another in Paris in 1939. While the French exhibition was less successful, the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> purchased a painting from Kahlo, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frame_(painting)" title="The Frame (painting)">The Frame</a></i>, making her the first Mexican artist to be featured in their collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> Throughout the 1940s, Kahlo participated in exhibitions in Mexico and the United States and worked as an art teacher. She taught at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Escuela_Nacional_de_Pintura,_Escultura_y_Grabado" title="Escuela Nacional de Pintura, Escultura y Grabado">Escuela Nacional de Pintura, Escultura y Grabado</a> ("<i>La Esmeralda</i>") and was a founding member of the <i>Seminario de Cultura Mexicana</i>. Kahlo's always-fragile health began to decline in the same decade. She had her first solo exhibition in Mexico in 1953, shortly before her death in 1954 at the age of 47.
</p><p>Kahlo's work as an artist remained relatively unknown until the late 1970s, when her work was rediscovered by art historians and political activists. By the early 1990s, not only had she become a recognized figure in art history, but she was also regarded as an icon for <a href="/wiki/Chicano_Movement" title="Chicano Movement">Chicanos</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a> movement, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LGBTQ%2B" title="LGBTQ+">LGBTQ+</a> movement. Kahlo's work has been celebrated internationally as emblematic of Mexican national and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Mexico" title="Indigenous peoples of Mexico">indigenous</a> traditions and by feminists for what is seen as its uncompromising depiction of the female experience and form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-broude_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-broude-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Personal_life"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Personal life</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#1907–1924:_Family_and_childhood"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">1907–1924: Family and childhood</span></a></li>

</li>

</li>

</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_career">Artistic career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo,_June_15,_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4495" data-file-width="3508" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_June_15%2C_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_June_15%2C_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_June_15%2C_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_June_15%2C_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_June_15%2C_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_June_15%2C_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Frida_Kahlo,_June_15,_1919_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kahlo on 15 June 1919, aged 11</div></div></div>
<p>Kahlo enjoyed art from an early age, receiving drawing instruction from printmaker Fernando Fernández (who was her father's friend)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori200220Burrus2005200_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori200220Burrus2005200-7">[7]</a></sup> and filling notebooks with sketches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199020_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199020-8">[8]</a></sup> In 1925, she began to work outside of school to help her family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199021_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199021-9">[9]</a></sup> After briefly working as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stenographer" title="Stenographer">stenographer</a>, she became a paid engraving apprentice for Fernández.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera200226–40_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera200226–40-10">[10]</a></sup> He was impressed by her talent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200312_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200312-11">[11]</a></sup> although she did not consider art as a career at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199020_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199020-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>A severe bus accident at the age of 18 left Kahlo in lifelong pain. Confined to bed for three months following the accident, Kahlo began to paint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12">[12]</a></sup> She started to consider a career as a <a href="/wiki/Medical_illustration" title="Medical illustration">medical illustrator</a>, as well, which would combine her interests in science and art. Her mother provided her with a specially-made <a href="/wiki/Easel" title="Easel">easel</a>, which enabled her to paint in bed, and her father lent her some of his oil paints. She had a mirror placed above the easel, so that she could see herself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200317–18Herrera200262–63Burrus2005_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200317–18Herrera200262–63Burrus2005-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12">[12]</a></sup> Painting became a way for Kahlo to explore questions of identity and existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005201Ankori2002101–102_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005201Ankori2002101–102-14">[14]</a></sup> She explained, "I paint myself because I am often alone and I am the subject I know best."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12">[12]</a></sup> She later stated that the accident and the isolating recovery period made her desire "to begin again, painting things just as [she] saw them with [her] own eyes and nothing more."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera200275_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera200275-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the paintings Kahlo made during this time were portraits of herself, her sisters, and her schoolfriends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200321Herrera200264_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200321Herrera200264-16">[16]</a></sup> Her early paintings and correspondence show that she drew inspiration especially from European artists, in particular Renaissance masters such as <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronzino" title="Bronzino">Bronzino</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200514Barson200558_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200514Barson200558-17">[17]</a></sup> and from <i><a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a></i> movements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neue_Sachlichkeit" title="Neue Sachlichkeit">Neue Sachlichkeit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori2002105–108Burrus200569_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori2002105–108Burrus200569-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>On moving to Morelos in 1929 with her husband Rivera, Kahlo was inspired by the city of <a href="/wiki/Cuernavaca" title="Cuernavaca">Cuernavaca</a> where they lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUdall200311_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUdall200311-19">[19]</a></sup> She changed her artistic style and increasingly drew inspiration from Mexican folk art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200515–17Kettenmann200320–25_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200515–17Kettenmann200320–25-20">[20]</a></sup> Art historian Andrea Kettenmann states that she may have been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Adolfo_Best_Maugard" title="Adolfo Best Maugard">Adolfo Best Maugard</a>'s treatise on the subject, for she incorporated many of the characteristics that he outlined – for example, the lack of perspective and the combining of elements from pre-Columbian and colonial periods of Mexican art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200324–25_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200324–25-21">[21]</a></sup> Her identification with <i><a href="/wiki/La_Raza" title="La Raza">La Raza</a></i>, the people of Mexico, and her profound interest in its culture remained important facets of her art throughout the rest of her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002109–113Zamora199078–80Ankori2002144–145_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002109–113Zamora199078–80Ankori2002144–145-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_in_the_United_States">Work in the United States</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="526" decoding="async" height="373" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg/250px-Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg/375px-Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg/500px-Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_-_1926.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kahlo in 1926</div></div></div>
<p>When Kahlo and Rivera moved to San Francisco in 1930, Kahlo was introduced to American artists such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Weston" title="Edward Weston">Edward Weston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Stackpole" title="Ralph Stackpole">Ralph Stackpole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_L._Pflueger" title="Timothy L. Pflueger">Timothy L. Pflueger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nickolas_Muray" title="Nickolas Muray">Nickolas Muray</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002117–125Zamora199042–43BlockHoffman-Jeep1998–19998_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002117–125Zamora199042–43BlockHoffman-Jeep1998–19998-23">[23]</a></sup> The six months spent in San Francisco were a productive period for Kahlo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002117–125Zamora199042–43Kettenmann200332_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002117–125Zamora199042–43Kettenmann200332-24">[24]</a></sup> who further developed the folk art style she had adopted in Cuernavaca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005203_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005203-25">[25]</a></sup> In addition to painting portraits of several new acquaintances,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002118–125Kettenmann200327_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002118–125Kettenmann200327-26">[26]</a></sup> she made <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frieda_and_Diego_Rivera_(painting)" title="Frieda and Diego Rivera (painting)">Frieda and Diego Rivera</a></i> (1931), a double portrait based on their wedding photograph,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002124–127Kettenmann200331Ankori2002140–145_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002124–127Kettenmann200331Ankori2002140–145-27">[27]</a></sup> and <i>The Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Luther_Burbank" title="Luther Burbank">Luther Burbank</a></i> (1931), which depicted the eponymous horticulturist as a hybrid between a human and a plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002123–125_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002123–125-28">[28]</a></sup> Although she still publicly presented herself as simply Rivera's spouse rather than as an artist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002117–125Marnham1998234–235_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002117–125Marnham1998234–235-29">[29]</a></sup> she participated for the first time in an exhibition, when <i>Frieda and Diego Rivera</i> was included in the Sixth Annual Exhibition of the San Francisco Society of Women Artists in the <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honor_(museum)" title="Legion of Honor (museum)">Palace of the Legion of Honor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>On moving to Detroit with Rivera, Kahlo experienced numerous health problems related to a failed pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002133–160Burrus2005201Zamora199046Kettenmann200332Ankori201387–94_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002133–160Burrus2005201Zamora199046Kettenmann200332Ankori201387–94-32">[32]</a></sup> Despite these health problems, as well as her dislike for the capitalist culture of the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200336_for_quote_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200336_for_quote-33">[33]</a></sup> Kahlo's time in the city was beneficial for her artistic expression. She experimented with different techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etching</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescos</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199046_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199046-34">[34]</a></sup> and her paintings began to show a stronger narrative style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> She also began placing emphasis on the themes of "terror, suffering, wounds, and pain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199046_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199046-34">[34]</a></sup> Despite the popularity of the mural in Mexican art at the time, she adopted a diametrically opposed medium, votive images or <i><a href="/wiki/Retablo" title="Retablo">retablos</a></i>, religious paintings made on small metal sheets by amateur artists to thank saints for their blessings during a calamity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005202Kettenmann200335–36_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005202Kettenmann200335–36-36">[36]</a></sup> Amongst the works she made in the <i>retablo</i> manner in Detroit are <i>Henry Ford Hospital</i> (1932), <i>My Birth</i> (1932), and <i>Self-Portrait on the Border of Mexico and the United States</i> (1932).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199046_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199046-34">[34]</a></sup> While none of Kahlo's works were featured in exhibitions in Detroit, she gave an interview to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit_News" title="Detroit News">Detroit News</a></i> on her art; the article was condescendingly titled "Wife of the Master Mural Painter Gleefully Dabbles in Works of Art".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBilek201214_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBilek201214-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Mexico_City_and_international_recognition">Return to Mexico City and international recognition</span></h3>
<p>Upon returning to Mexico City in 1934 Kahlo made no new paintings, and only two in the following year, due to health complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori2002160_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori2002160-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1937 and 1938, however, Kahlo's artistic career was extremely productive, following her divorce and then reconciliation with Rivera. She painted more "than she had done in all her eight previous years of marriage", creating such works as <i>My Nurse and I</i> (1937), <i><a href="/wiki/Memory,_the_Heart" title="Memory, the Heart">Memory, the Heart</a></i> (1937), <i>Four Inhabitants of Mexico</i> (1938), and <i><a href="/wiki/What_the_Water_Gave_Me_(painting)" title="What the Water Gave Me (painting)">What the Water Gave Me</a></i> (1938).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002215_for_quoteZamora199056Kettenmann200345_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002215_for_quoteZamora199056Kettenmann200345-39">[39]</a></sup> Although she was still unsure about her work, the <a href="/wiki/National_Autonomous_University_of_Mexico" title="National Autonomous University of Mexico">National Autonomous University of Mexico</a> exhibited some of her paintings in early 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002226_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002226-40">[40]</a></sup> She made her first significant sale in the summer of 1938 when film star and art collector <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Robinson" title="Edward G. Robinson">Edward G. Robinson</a> purchased four paintings at $200 each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002226_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002226-40">[40]</a></sup> Even greater recognition followed when French Surrealist <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Breton" title="André Breton">André Breton</a> visited Rivera in April 1938. He was impressed by Kahlo, immediately claiming her as a surrealist and describing her work as "a ribbon around a bomb".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201133–34_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201133–34-41">[41]</a></sup> He not only promised to arrange for her paintings to be exhibited in Paris but also wrote to his friend and art dealer, <a href="/wiki/Julien_Levy" title="Julien Levy">Julien Levy</a>, who invited her to hold her first solo exhibition at his gallery on the East 57th Street in Manhattan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200345Mahon201133–34_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200345Mahon201133–34-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diego_Rivera,_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Diego_Rivera%2C_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg/260px-Diego_Rivera%2C_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Diego_Rivera%2C_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg/390px-Diego_Rivera%2C_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Diego_Rivera%2C_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg/520px-Diego_Rivera%2C_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diego_Rivera,_Frida_Kahlo_%26_Anson_Goodyear.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rivera, Kahlo, and <a href="/wiki/Anson_Goodyear" title="Anson Goodyear">Anson Goodyear</a></div></div></div>
<p>In October, Kahlo traveled alone to New York, where her colorful Mexican dress "caused a sensation" and made her seen as "the height of exotica".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201133–34_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201133–34-41">[41]</a></sup> The exhibition opening in November was attended by famous figures such as <a href="/wiki/Georgia_O%27Keeffe" title="Georgia O'Keeffe">Georgia O'Keeffe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clare_Boothe_Luce" title="Clare Boothe Luce">Clare Boothe Luce</a> and received much positive attention in the press, although many critics adopted a condescending tone in their reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–232Mahon201134–35_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–232Mahon201134–35-43">[43]</a></sup> For example, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> wrote that "Little Frida's pictures ... had the daintiness of miniatures, the vivid reds, and yellows of Mexican tradition and the playfully bloody fancy of an unsentimental child".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–232_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–232-44">[44]</a></sup> Despite the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, Kahlo sold half of the 25 paintings presented in the exhibition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005204_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005204-45">[45]</a></sup> She also received commissions from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A._Conger_Goodyear" title="A. Conger Goodyear">A. Conger Goodyear</a>, then the president of the MoMA, and Clare Boothe Luce, for whom she painted a portrait of Luce's friend, socialite <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Hale" title="Dorothy Hale">Dorothy Hale</a>, who had committed suicide by jumping from her apartment building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–235_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–235-46">[46]</a></sup> During the three months she spent in New York, Kahlo painted very little, instead focusing on enjoying the city to the extent that her fragile health allowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–240_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–240-47">[47]</a></sup> She also had several affairs, continuing the one with Nickolas Muray and engaging in ones with Levy and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edgar_Kaufmann,_Jr." title="Edgar Kaufmann, Jr.">Edgar Kaufmann, Jr.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–240Ankori2002193_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002230–240Ankori2002193-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1939, Kahlo sailed to Paris to follow up on André Breton's invitation to stage an exhibition of her work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–243_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–243-49">[49]</a></sup> When she arrived, she found that he had not cleared her paintings from the customs and no longer even owned a gallery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–245_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–245-50">[50]</a></sup> With the aid of <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Duchamp" title="Marcel Duchamp">Marcel Duchamp</a>, she was able to arrange for an exhibition at the Renou et Colle Gallery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–245_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–245-50">[50]</a></sup> Further problems arose when the gallery refused to show all but two of Kahlo's paintings, considering them too shocking for audiences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002241–245_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002241–245-51">[51]</a></sup> and Breton insisted that they be shown alongside photographs by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_Alvarez_Bravo" title="Manuel Alvarez Bravo">Manuel Alvarez Bravo</a>, pre-Columbian sculptures, 18th- and 19th-century Mexican portraits, and what she considered "junk": sugar skulls, toys, and other items he had bought from Mexican markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201145_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201145-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exhibition opened in March, but received much less attention than she had received in the United States, partly due to the looming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, and made a loss financially, which led Kahlo to cancel a planned exhibition in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–250_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–250-53">[53]</a></sup> Regardless, the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> purchased <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frame_(painting)" title="The Frame (painting)">The Frame</a></i>, making her the first Mexican artist to be featured in their collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–250Mahon201145_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–250Mahon201145-54">[54]</a></sup> She was also warmly received by other Parisian artists, such as <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joan_Mir%C3%B3" title="Joan Miró">Joan Miró</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201145_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201145-52">[52]</a></sup> as well as the fashion world, with designer <a href="/wiki/Elsa_Schiaparelli" title="Elsa Schiaparelli">Elsa Schiaparelli</a> designing a dress inspired by her and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vogue_Paris" title="Vogue Paris">Vogue Paris</a></i> featuring her on its pages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–250_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351–52Herrera2002241–250-53">[53]</a></sup> However, her overall opinion of Paris and the Surrealists remained negative; in a letter to Muray, she called them "this bunch of coocoo lunatics and very stupid surrealists"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201145_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201145-52">[52]</a></sup> who "are so crazy 'intellectual' and rotten that I can't even stand them anymore".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200351-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, Kahlo's paintings continued to raise interest. In 1941, her works were featured at the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Contemporary_Art,_Boston" title="Institute of Contemporary Art, Boston">Institute of Contemporary Art</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, and, in the following year, she participated in two high-profile exhibitions in New York, the <i>Twentieth-Century Portraits</i> exhibition at the MoMA and the Surrealists' <i>First Papers of Surrealism</i> exhibition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005220–221_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005220–221-56">[56]</a></sup> In 1943, she was included in the <i>Mexican Art Today</i> exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a> and <i>Women Artists</i> at <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Guggenheim" title="Peggy Guggenheim">Peggy Guggenheim</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_This_Century_gallery" title="The Art of This Century gallery">The Art of This Century gallery</a> in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–318Zamora1990137Burrus2005220–221_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–318Zamora1990137Burrus2005220–221-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kahlo gained more appreciation for her art in Mexico as well. She became a founding member of the Seminario de Cultura Mexicana, a group of twenty-five artists commissioned by the Ministry of Public Education in 1942 to spread public knowledge of Mexican culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200361–62Herrera2002321–322_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200361–62Herrera2002321–322-58">[58]</a></sup> As a member, she took part in planning exhibitions and attended a conference on art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200361–62_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200361–62-59">[59]</a></sup> In Mexico City, her paintings were featured in two exhibitions on Mexican art that were staged at the English-language Benjamin Franklin Library in 1943 and 1944. She was invited to participate in "Salon de la Flor", an exhibition presented at the annual flower exposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–320_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–320-60">[60]</a></sup> An article by Rivera on Kahlo's art was also published in the journal published by the Seminario de Cultura Mexicana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005221Herrera2002321–322_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005221Herrera2002321–322-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External images</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="image icon" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/culturalinstitute/beta/asset/the-broken-column/EgGMbMFBQrAe3Q" rel="nofollow"><i>The Broken Column</i> (1944)</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="image icon" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.wikiart.org/en/frida-kahlo/moses-1945" rel="nofollow"><i>Moses</i> (1945)</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="image icon" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/culturalinstitute/beta/asset/without-hope/rgHBxF7ESC2qmQ" rel="nofollow"><i>Without Hope</i> (1945)</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="image icon" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.wikiart.org/en/frida-kahlo/tree-of-hope-remain-strong-1946" rel="nofollow"><i>Tree of Hope, Stand Fast</i> (1946)</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 1943, Kahlo accepted a teaching position at the recently reformed, nationalistic <a href="/wiki/Escuela_Nacional_de_Pintura,_Escultura_y_Grabado_%22La_Esmeralda%22" title='Escuela Nacional de Pintura, Escultura y Grabado "La Esmeralda"'>Escuela Nacional de Pintura, Escultura y Grabado "La Esmeralda."</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–96_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–96-62">[62]</a></sup> She encouraged her students to treat her in an informal and non-hierarchical way and taught them to appreciate Mexican popular culture and folk art and to derive their subjects from the street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–96Kettenmann200363–67Herrera2002330–332Burrus2005205_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–96Kettenmann200363–67Herrera2002330–332Burrus2005205-63">[63]</a></sup> When her health problems made it difficult for her to commute to the school in Mexico City, she began to hold her lessons at La Casa Azul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–96Kettenmann200363–68_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–96Kettenmann200363–68-64">[64]</a></sup> Four of her students – <a href="/wiki/Fanny_Rabel" title="Fanny Rabel">Fanny Rabel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arturo_Garc%C3%ADa_Bustos" title="Arturo García Bustos">Arturo García Bustos</a>, Guillermo Monroy, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arturo_Estrada" title="Arturo Estrada">Arturo Estrada</a> – became devotees, and were referred to as "Los Fridos" for their enthusiasm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–97Kettenmann200363–68_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199095–97Kettenmann200363–68-65">[65]</a></sup> Kahlo secured three mural commissions for herself and her students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002335–343_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002335–343-66">[66]</a></sup> In 1944, they painted La Rosita, a <i><a href="/wiki/Pulqueria" title="Pulqueria">pulqueria</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Coyoac%C3%A1n" title="Coyoacán">Coyoacán</a>. In 1945, the government commissioned them to paint murals for a Coyoacán launderette as part of a national scheme to help poor women who made their living as laundresses. The same year, the group created murals for Posada del Sol, a hotel in Mexico City. However, it was destroyed soon after completion as the hotel's owner did not like it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Kahlo struggled to make a living from her art until the mid to late 1940s, as she refused to adapt her style to suit her clients' wishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–334_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–334-67">[67]</a></sup> She received two commissions from the Mexican government in the early 1940s. She did not complete the first one, possibly due to her dislike of the subject, and the second commission was rejected by the commissioning body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–334_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–334-67">[67]</a></sup> Nevertheless, she had regular private clients, such as engineer Eduardo Morillo Safa, who ordered more than thirty portraits of family members over the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–334_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002316–334-67">[67]</a></sup> Her financial situation improved when she received a 5000-peso national prize for her painting <i>Moses</i> (1945) in 1946 and when <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Fridas" title="The Two Fridas">The Two Fridas</a></i> was purchased by the <a href="/wiki/Museo_de_Arte_Moderno" title="Museo de Arte Moderno">Museo de Arte Moderno</a> in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002320–322_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002320–322-68">[68]</a></sup> According to art historian Andrea Kettenmann, by the mid-1940s, her paintings were "featured in the majority of group exhibitions in Mexico". Further, Martha Zamora wrote that she could "sell whatever she was currently painting; sometimes incomplete pictures were purchased right off the easel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990100Kettenmann200362_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990100Kettenmann200362-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span></h3>
<p>Even as Kahlo was gaining recognition in Mexico, her health was declining rapidly, and an attempted surgery to support her spine failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359-70">[70]</a></sup> Her paintings from this period include <i>Broken Column</i> (1944), <i>Without Hope</i> (1945), <i>Tree of Hope, Stand Fast</i> (1946), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wounded_Deer" title="The Wounded Deer">The Wounded Deer</a></i> (1946), reflecting her poor physical state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359-70">[70]</a></sup> During her last years, Kahlo was mostly confined to the Casa Azul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002389–400_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002389–400-71">[71]</a></sup> She painted mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Still_lifes" title="Still lifes">still lifes</a>, portraying fruit and flowers with political symbols such as flags or doves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379–80Herrera2002397–398_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379–80Herrera2002397–398-72">[72]</a></sup> She was concerned about being able to portray her political convictions, stating that "I have a great restlessness about my paintings. Mainly because I want to make it useful to the revolutionary communist movement... until now I have managed simply an honest expression of my own self ... I must struggle with all my strength to ensure that the little positive my health allows me to do also benefits the Revolution, the only real reason to live."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200380_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200380-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> She also altered her painting style: her brushstrokes, previously delicate and careful, were now hastier, her use of color more brash, and the overall style more intense and feverish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann2003Herrera2002398–399_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann2003Herrera2002398–399-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Photographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lola_Alvarez_Bravo" title="Lola Alvarez Bravo">Lola Alvarez Bravo</a> understood that Kahlo did not have much longer to live, and thus staged her first solo exhibition in Mexico at the Galería Arte Contemporaneo in April 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990138Herrera2002405–410Burrus2005206_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990138Herrera2002405–410Burrus2005206-76">[76]</a></sup> Though Kahlo was initially not due to attend the opening, as her doctors had prescribed bed rest for her, she ordered her four-poster bed to be moved from her home to the gallery. To the surprise of the guests, she arrived in an ambulance and was carried on a stretcher to the bed, where she stayed for the duration of the party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990138Herrera2002405–410Burrus2005206_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990138Herrera2002405–410Burrus2005206-76">[76]</a></sup> The exhibition was a notable cultural event in Mexico and also received attention in mainstream press around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002405–410_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002405–410-77">[77]</a></sup> The same year, the <a href="/wiki/Tate_Britain" title="Tate Britain">Tate Gallery</a>'s exhibition on Mexican art in London featured five of her paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005223_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005223-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954, Kahlo was again hospitalized in April and May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990138_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990138-79">[79]</a></sup> That spring, she resumed painting after a one-year interval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430-80">[80]</a></sup> Her last paintings include the political <i><a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> Will Give Health to the Sick</i> (c. 1954) and <i>Frida and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalin" title="Stalin">Stalin</a></i> (c. 1954) and the still-life <i>Viva La Vida</i> (1954).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990130Kettenmann200380–82_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990130Kettenmann200380–82-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_and_influences">Style and influences</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_paintings_by_Frida_Kahlo" title="List of paintings by Frida Kahlo">List of paintings by Frida Kahlo</a></div>
<p>Estimates vary on how many paintings Kahlo made during her life, with figures ranging from fewer than 150<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200511_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200511-82">[82]</a></sup> to around 200.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeffebach2006174Cooey199495_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeffebach2006174Cooey199495-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerreraOxfordArt_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-HerreraOxfordArt-84">[84]</a></sup> Her earliest paintings, which she made in the mid-1920s, show influence from Renaissance masters and European avant-garde artists such as <a href="/wiki/Amedeo_Modigliani" title="Amedeo Modigliani">Amedeo Modigliani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200514Barson200558Kettenmann200322_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200514Barson200558Kettenmann200322-85">[85]</a></sup> Towards the end of the decade, Kahlo derived more inspiration from Mexican folk art,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200454_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200454-86">[86]</a></sup> drawn to its elements of "fantasy, naivety, and fascination with violence and death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerreraOxfordArt_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-HerreraOxfordArt-84">[84]</a></sup> The style she developed mixed reality with surrealistic elements and often depicted pain and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Kahlo's earliest champions was Surrealist artist André Breton, who claimed her as part of the movement as an artist who had supposedly developed her style "in <i>total ignorance</i> of the ideas that motivated the activities of my friends and myself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori20022_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori20022-88">[88]</a></sup> This was echoed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bertram_D._Wolfe" title="Bertram D. Wolfe">Bertram D. Wolfe</a>, who wrote that Kahlo's was a "sort of 'naïve' Surrealism, which she invented for herself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201133_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201133-89">[89]</a></sup> Although Breton regarded her as mostly a feminine force within the Surrealist movement, Kahlo brought postcolonial questions and themes to the forefront of her brand of Surrealism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahon201133–49Dexter200520–22_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahon201133–49Dexter200520–22-90">[90]</a></sup> Breton also described Kahlo's work as "wonderfully situated at the point of intersection between the political (philosophical) line and the artistic line".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> While she subsequently participated in Surrealist exhibitions, she stated that she "detest[ed] Surrealism", which to her was "bourgeois art" and not "true art that the people hope from the artist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–199112_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–199112-92">[92]</a></sup> Some art historians have disagreed whether her work should be classified as belonging to the movement at all. According to Andrea Kettenmann, Kahlo was a <a href="/wiki/Symbolism_(arts)" title="Symbolism (arts)">symbolist</a> concerned more in portraying her inner experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200370_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200370-93">[93]</a></sup> Emma Dexter has argued that, as Kahlo derived her mix of fantasy and reality mainly from Aztec mythology and Mexican culture instead of Surrealism, it is more appropriate to consider her paintings as having more in common with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magical_realism" title="Magical realism">magical realism</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/New_Objectivity" title="New Objectivity">New Objectivity</a>. It combined reality and fantasy and employed similar style to Kahlo's, such as flattened perspective, clearly outlined characters and bright colours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200521–22_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200521–22-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexicanidad"><i>Mexicanidad</i></span></h3>
<p>Similarly to many other contemporary Mexican artists, Kahlo was heavily influenced by <i><a href="/wiki/Mexicayotl" title="Mexicayotl">Mexicanidad</a></i>, a romantic nationalism that had developed in the aftermath of the revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200324–28Helland1990–19918–13Bakewell1993167–169_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200324–28Helland1990–19918–13Bakewell1993167–169-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerreraOxfordArt_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-HerreraOxfordArt-84">[84]</a></sup> The <i>Mexicanidad</i> movement claimed to resist the "mindset of cultural inferiority" created by colonialism, and placed special importance on indigenous cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993167–168Cooey199495Dexter200520–21_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993167–168Cooey199495Dexter200520–21-96">[96]</a></sup> Before the revolution, Mexican folk culture – a mixture of indigenous and European elements – was disparaged by the elite, who claimed to have purely European ancestry and regarded Europe as the definition of civilization which Mexico should imitate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson119_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson119-97">[97]</a></sup> Kahlo's artistic ambition was to paint for the Mexican people, and she stated that she wished "to be worthy, with my paintings, of the people to whom I belong and to the ideas which strengthen me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–199112_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–199112-92">[92]</a></sup> To enforce this image, she preferred to conceal the education she had received in art from her father and Ferdinand Fernandez and at the preparatory school. Instead, she cultivated an image of herself as a "self-taught and naive artist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarson200576_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarson200576-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Kahlo began her career as an artist in the 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Mexican_muralism" title="Mexican muralism">muralists</a> dominated the Mexican art scene. They created large public pieces in the vein of Renaissance masters and Russian <a href="/wiki/Socialist_realism" title="Socialist realism">socialist realists</a>: they usually depicted masses of people, and their political messages were easy to decipher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell2001316Deffebach2006171_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell2001316Deffebach2006171-99">[99]</a></sup> Although she was close to muralists such as Rivera, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Clemente_Orozco" title="José Clemente Orozco">José Clemente Orozco</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Alfaro_Siquieros" title="David Alfaro Siquieros">David Alfaro Siquieros</a> and shared their commitment to socialism and Mexican nationalism, the majority of Kahlo's paintings were self-portraits of relatively small size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell2001316–317_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell2001316–317-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerreraOxfordArt_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-HerreraOxfordArt-84">[84]</a></sup> Particularly in the 1930s, her style was especially indebted to <a href="/wiki/Votive_paintings_of_Mexico" title="Votive paintings of Mexico">votive paintings</a> or <i>retablos</i>, which were postcard-sized religious images made by amateur artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993168–169Castro-Sethness2004–200521Deffebach176–177Dexter200516_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993168–169Castro-Sethness2004–200521Deffebach176–177Dexter200516-101">[101]</a></sup> Their purpose was to thank saints for their protection during a calamity, and they normally depicted an event, such as an illness or an accident, from which its commissioner had been saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastro-Sethness2004–200521Kettenmann200335_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastro-Sethness2004–200521Kettenmann200335-102">[102]</a></sup> The focus was on the figures depicted, and they seldom featured a realistic perspective or detailed background, thus distilling the event to its essentials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200335_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200335-103">[103]</a></sup> Kahlo had an extensive collection of approximately 2,000 <i>retablos</i>, which she displayed on the walls of La Casa Azul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastro-Sethness2004–200521Barson200565Bakewell1993173–174Cooey199496–97_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastro-Sethness2004–200521Barson200565Bakewell1993173–174Cooey199496–97-104">[104]</a></sup> According to Laura Mulvey and Peter Wollen, the <i>retablo</i> format enabled Kahlo to "develop the limits of the purely iconic and allowed her to use narrative and allegory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993173,_quotes_Mulvey_&amp;_Wollen's_1982_catalogue_essay_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993173,_quotes_Mulvey_&amp;_Wollen's_1982_catalogue_essay-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of Kahlo's self-portraits mimic the classic bust-length portraits that were fashionable during the colonial era, but they subverted the format by depicting their subject as less attractive than in reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993168–169Castro-Sethness2004–200521Deffebach2006176–177_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993168–169Castro-Sethness2004–200521Deffebach2006176–177-106">[106]</a></sup> She concentrated more frequently on this format towards the end of the 1930s, thus reflecting changes in Mexican society. Increasingly disillusioned by the legacy of the revolution and struggling to cope with the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, Mexicans were abandoning the ethos of socialism for individualism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200517_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200517-107">[107]</a></sup> This was reflected by the "personality cults", which developed around Mexican film stars such as <a href="/wiki/Dolores_del_R%C3%ADo" title="Dolores del Río">Dolores del Río</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200517_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200517-107">[107]</a></sup> According to Schaefer, Kahlo's "mask-like self-portraits echo the contemporaneous fascination with the cinematic close-up of feminine beauty, as well as the mystique of female otherness expressed in <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">film noir</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200517_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200517-107">[107]</a></sup> By always repeating the same facial features, Kahlo drew from the depiction of goddesses and saints in indigenous and Catholic cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeffebach2006177–178_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeffebach2006177–178-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Out of specific Mexican folk artists, Kahlo was especially influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hermenegildo_Bustos" title="Hermenegildo Bustos">Hermenegildo Bustos</a>, whose works portrayed Mexican culture and peasant life, and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Guadalupe_Posada" title="José Guadalupe Posada">José Guadalupe Posada</a>, who depicted accidents and crime in satiric manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarson200559,_73Cooey199498_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarson200559,_73Cooey199498-109">[109]</a></sup> She also derived inspiration from the works of <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Bosch" title="Hieronymus Bosch">Hieronymus Bosch</a>, whom she called a "man of genius", and <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Bruegel the Elder">Pieter Bruegel the Elder</a>, whose focus on peasant life was similar to her own interest in the Mexican people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarson200558–59_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarson200558–59-110">[110]</a></sup> Another influence was the poet <a href="/wiki/Rosario_Castellanos" title="Rosario Castellanos">Rosario Castellanos</a>, whose poems often chronicle a woman's lot in the patriarchal Mexican society, a concern with the female body, and tell stories of immense physical and emotional pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200454_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200454-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism_and_iconography">Symbolism and iconography</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_(self_portrait).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="277" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1e/Frida_Kahlo_%28self_portrait%29.jpg/220px-Frida_Kahlo_%28self_portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1e/Frida_Kahlo_%28self_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_(self_portrait).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Self-Portrait_with_Thorn_Necklace_and_Hummingbird" title="Self-Portrait with Thorn Necklace and Hummingbird">Self-Portrait with Thorn Necklace and Hummingbird</a></i> (1940), <a href="/wiki/Harry_Ransom_Center" title="Harry Ransom Center">Harry Ransom Center</a></div></div></div>
<p>Kahlo's paintings often feature root imagery, with roots growing out of her body to tie her to the ground. This reflects in a positive sense the theme of personal growth; in a negative sense of being trapped in a particular place, time and situation; and in an ambiguous sense of how memories of the past influence the present for good and/or ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200455_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200455-111">[111]</a></sup> In <i>My Grandparents and I</i>, Kahlo painted herself as a ten-year old, holding a ribbon that grows from an ancient tree that bears the portraits of her grandparents and other ancestors while her left foot is a tree trunk growing out of the ground, reflecting Kahlo's view of humanity's unity with the earth and her own sense of unity with Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200457_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200457-112">[112]</a></sup> In Kahlo's paintings, trees serve as symbols of hope, of strength and of a continuity that transcends generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200458_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200458-113">[113]</a></sup> Additionally, hair features as a symbol of growth and of the feminine in Kahlo's paintings and in <i>Self-Portrait with Cropped Hair</i>, Kahlo painted herself wearing a man's suit and shorn of her long hair, which she had just cut off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200455–56_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200455–56-114">[114]</a></sup> Kahlo holds the scissors with one hand menacingly close to her genitals, which can be interpreted as a threat to Rivera – whose frequent unfaithfulness infuriated her – and/or a threat to harm her own body like she has attacked her own hair, a sign of the way that women often project their fury against others onto themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200456_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200456-115">[115]</a></sup> Moreover, the picture reflects Kahlo's frustration not only with Rivera, but also her unease with the patriarchal values of Mexico as the scissors symbolize a malevolent sense of masculinity that threatens to "cut up" women, both metaphorically and literally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200456_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200456-115">[115]</a></sup> In Mexico, the traditional Spanish values of <i>machismo</i> were widely embraced, but Kahlo was always uncomfortable with <i>machismo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200456_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200456-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>As she suffered for the rest of her life from the bus accident in her youth, Kahlo spent much of her life in hospitals and undergoing surgery, much of it performed by quacks who Kahlo believed could restore her back to where she had been before the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200457_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200457-112">[112]</a></sup> Many of Kahlo's paintings are concerned with medical imagery, which is presented in terms of pain and hurt, featuring Kahlo bleeding and displaying her open wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200457_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200457-112">[112]</a></sup> Many of Kahlo's medical paintings, especially dealing with childbirth and miscarriage, have a strong sense of guilt, of a sense of living one's life at the expense of another who has died so one might live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriis200458_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriis200458-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Kahlo featured herself and events from her life in her paintings, they were often ambiguous in meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooey199498–99_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooey199498–99-116">[116]</a></sup> She did not use them only to show her subjective experience but to raise questions about Mexican society and the construction of identity within it, particularly gender, race, and social class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993168–170Ankori200531_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993168–170Ankori200531-117">[117]</a></sup> Historian Liza Bakewell has stated that Kahlo "recognized the conflicts brought on by revolutionary ideology":
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>What was it to be a Mexican? – modern, yet pre-Columbian; young, yet old; anti-Catholic yet Catholic; Western, yet New World; developing, yet underdeveloped; independent, yet colonized; <i>mestizo</i>, yet not Spanish nor Indian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993169_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1993169-118">[118]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>To explore these questions through her art, Kahlo developed a complex iconography, extensively employing pre-Columbian and Christian symbols and mythology in her paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelland19908–13_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelland19908–13-119">[119]</a></sup> In most of her self-portraits, she depicts her face as mask-like, but surrounded by visual cues which allow the viewer to decipher deeper meanings for it. Aztec mythology features heavily in Kahlo's paintings in symbols including monkeys, skeletons, skulls, blood, and hearts; often, these symbols referred to the myths of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coatlicue" title="Coatlicue">Coatlicue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quetzalcoatl" title="Quetzalcoatl">Quetzalcoatl</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xolotl" title="Xolotl">Xolotl</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–19918–13Barson200556–79_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–19918–13Barson200556–79-120">[120]</a></sup> Other central elements that Kahlo derived from <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztec</a> mythology were hybridity and dualism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–19918–13Dexter200512–13Barson200564_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–19918–13Dexter200512–13Barson200564-121">[121]</a></sup> Many of her paintings depict opposites: life and death, pre-modernity and modernity, Mexican and European, male and female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–19918–13_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelland1990–19918–13-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to Aztec legends, Kahlo frequently depicted two central female figures from Mexican folklore in her paintings: <a href="/wiki/La_Llorona" title="La Llorona">La Llorona</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_Malinche" title="La Malinche">La Malinche</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori200531–43Barson200569–70_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori200531–43Barson200569–70-123">[123]</a></sup> as interlinked to the hard situations, the suffering, misfortune or judgement, as being calamitous, wretched or being "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Chingada" title="La Chingada">de la chingada</a></i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarson200560_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarson200560-124">[124]</a></sup> For example, when she painted herself following her miscarriage in Detroit in <i>Henry Ford Hospital</i> (1932), she shows herself as weeping, with dishevelled hair and an exposed heart, which are all considered part of the appearance of La Llorona, a woman who murdered her children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori2002149–163_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori2002149–163-125">[125]</a></sup> The painting was traditionally interpreted as simply a depiction of Kahlo's grief and pain over her failed pregnancies. But with the interpretation of the symbols in the painting and the information of Kahlo's actual views towards motherhood from her correspondence, the painting has been seen as depicting the unconventional and taboo choice of a woman remaining childless in Mexican society.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Kahlo often featured her own body in her paintings, presenting it in varying states and disguises: as wounded, broken, as a child, or clothed in different outfits, such as the Tehuana costume, a man's suit, or a European dress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooey199495–108Dexter200512Barson200558_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooey199495–108Dexter200512Barson200558-126">[126]</a></sup> She used her body as a metaphor to explore questions on societal roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooey199495–108Dexter200512_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooey199495–108Dexter200512-127">[127]</a></sup> Her paintings often depicted the female body in an unconventional manner, such as during miscarriages, and childbirth or cross-dressing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell1997725Cooey1994102Helland1990–199110Deffebach2006176Barson200558_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell1997725Cooey1994102Helland1990–199110Deffebach2006176Barson200558-128">[128]</a></sup> In depicting the female body in graphic manner, Kahlo positioned the viewer in the role of the voyeur, "making it virtually impossible for a viewer not to assume a consciously held position in response".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooey1994108_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooey1994108-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>
According to Nancy Cooey, Kahlo made herself through her paintings into "the main character of her own mythology, as a woman, as a Mexican, and as a suffering person ... She knew how to convert each into a symbol or sign capable of expressing the enormous spiritual resistance of humanity and its splendid sexuality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooey199499_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooey199499-130">[130]</a></sup> Similarly, Nancy Deffebach has stated that Kahlo "created herself as a subject who was female, Mexican, modern, and powerful", and who diverged from the usual dichotomy of roles of mother/whore allowed to women in Mexican society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeffebach2006172–178_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeffebach2006172–178-131">[131]</a></sup> Due to her gender and divergence from the muralist tradition, Kahlo's paintings were treated as less political and more naïve and subjective than those of her male counterparts up until the late 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori20021–3Cooey1994102Helland1990–19918–13_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori20021–3Cooey1994102Helland1990–19918–13-132">[132]</a></sup> According to art historian Joan Borsa,</p><blockquote><p>the critical reception of her exploration of subjectivity and personal history has all too frequently denied or de-emphasized the politics involved in examining one's own location, inheritances and social conditions ... Critical responses continue to gloss over Kahlo's reworking of the personal, ignoring or minimizing her interrogation of sexuality, sexual difference, marginality, cultural identity, female subjectivity, politics and power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDexter200511_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDexter200511-82">[82]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span id="1907.E2.80.931924:_Family_and_childhood"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1907–1924:_Family_and_childhood">1907–1924: Family and childhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde,_Adriana,_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3693" data-file-width="3524" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde%2C_Adriana%2C_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde%2C_Adriana%2C_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde%2C_Adriana%2C_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde%2C_Adriana%2C_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde%2C_Adriana%2C_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde%2C_Adriana%2C_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guillermo_Kahlo_-_Matilde,_Adriana,_Frida_and_Cristina_Kahlo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kahlo (on the right) and her sisters <a href="/wiki/Cristina_Kahlo" title="Cristina Kahlo">Cristina</a>, Matilde, and Adriana, photographed by their father, 1916</div></div></div>
<p>Magdalena Carmen Frida Kahlo y Calderón<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[a]</a></sup> was born on 6 July 1907 in <a href="/wiki/Coyoac%C3%A1n" title="Coyoacán">Coyoacán</a>, a village on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005199Herrera20023–4Ankori200217_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005199Herrera20023–4Ankori200217-135">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-136">[135]</a></sup> Kahlo stated that she was born at the family home, <a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo_Museum" title="Frida Kahlo Museum">La Casa Azul</a> (The Blue House), but according to the official birth registry, the birth took place at the nearby home of her maternal grandmother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199015_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199015-137">[136]</a></sup> Kahlo's parents were photographer <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Kahlo" title="Guillermo Kahlo">Guillermo Kahlo</a> (1871–1941) and Matilde Calderón y González (1876–1932), and they were thirty-six and thirty, respectively, when they had her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Originally from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, Guillermo had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_immigration_to_Mexico" title="German immigration to Mexico">immigrated to Mexico</a> in 1891, after <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> caused by an accident ended his university studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera20024–9Ankori200217_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera20024–9Ankori200217-139">[138]</a></sup> Although Kahlo said her father was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> and her paternal grandparents were Jews from the city of <a href="/wiki/Arad,_Romania" title="Arad, Romania">Arad</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> this claim was challenged in 2006 by  a pair of German genealogists who found he was instead a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeffebach201552_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeffebach201552-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jerpost1_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-jerpost1-142">[141]</a></sup> Matilde was born in <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a> to an <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Mexico" title="Indigenous peoples of Mexico">Indigenous</a> father and a mother of <a href="/wiki/Spaniards_in_Mexico" title="Spaniards in Mexico">Spanish</a> descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera20024–9Ankori200217–18Burrus2005199_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera20024–9Ankori200217–18Burrus2005199-143">[142]</a></sup> In addition to Kahlo, the marriage produced daughters Matilde (<i>c.</i> 1898–1951), Adriana (<i>c.</i> 1902–1968), and <a href="/wiki/Cristina_Kahlo" title="Cristina Kahlo">Cristina</a> (<i>c.</i> 1908–1964).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera200210–11Ankori200218_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera200210–11Ankori200218-144">[143]</a></sup> She had two half-sisters from Guillermo's first marriage, María Luisa and Margarita, but they were raised in a convent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera200210–11Ankori200218Zamora199015–16_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera200210–11Ankori200218Zamora199015–16-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kahlo later described the atmosphere in her childhood home as often "very, very sad".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori200218_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori200218-146">[145]</a></sup> Both parents were often sick,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori200218Herrera200210–12_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori200218Herrera200210–12-147">[146]</a></sup> and their marriage was devoid of love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck200657_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck200657-148">[147]</a></sup> Her relationship with her mother, Matilde, was extremely tense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann20038–10Zamora199016Ankori200218Burrus2005199_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann20038–10Zamora199016Ankori200218Burrus2005199-149">[148]</a></sup> Kahlo described her mother as "kind, active and intelligent, but also calculating, cruel and fanatically religious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann20038–10Zamora199016Ankori200218Burrus2005199_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann20038–10Zamora199016Ankori200218Burrus2005199-149">[148]</a></sup> Her father Guillermo's photography business suffered greatly during the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a>, as the overthrown government had commissioned works from him, and the long civil war limited the number of private clients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori200218Herrera200210–12_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori200218Herrera200210–12-147">[146]</a></sup>
