<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo,_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5345" data-file-width="5418" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg/220px-Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg/330px-Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg/440px-Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo%2C_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toni_Frissell_-_Frida_Kahlo,_seated_next_to_an_agave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1937 photograph by <a href="/wiki/Toni_Frissell" title="Toni Frissell">Toni Frissell</a>, from a fashion shoot for <i><a href="/wiki/Vogue_(magazine)" title="Vogue (magazine)">Vogue</a></i></div></div></div><p>
After opening an exhibition in Paris, Kahlo sailed back to New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252-217">[215]</a></sup> She was eager to be reunited with Muray, but he decided to end their affair, as he had met another woman whom he was planning to marry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252Marnham290_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252Marnham290-218">[216]</a></sup> Kahlo traveled back to Mexico City, where Rivera requested a divorce from her. The exact reasons for his decision are unknown, but he stated publicly that it was merely a "matter of legal convenience in the style of modern times ... there are no sentimental, artistic, or economic reasons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199062_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199062-219">[217]</a></sup> According to their friends, the divorce was mainly caused by their mutual infidelities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252,_273–27Zamora199062–64Marnham290_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252,_273–27Zamora199062–64Marnham290-220">[218]</a></sup> He and Kahlo were granted a divorce in November 1939, but remained friendly; she continued to manage his finances and correspondence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252,_273–277_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002250–252,_273–277-221">[219]</a></sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style></p><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_House,_Mexico_City_(6998147374).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="3216" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frida_Kahlo_House%2C_Mexico_City_%286998147374%29.jpg/200px-Frida_Kahlo_House%2C_Mexico_City_%286998147374%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frida_Kahlo_House%2C_Mexico_City_%286998147374%29.jpg/300px-Frida_Kahlo_House%2C_Mexico_City_%286998147374%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frida_Kahlo_House%2C_Mexico_City_%286998147374%29.jpg/400px-Frida_Kahlo_House%2C_Mexico_City_%286998147374%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo_Museum" title="Frida Kahlo Museum">La Casa Azul</a>, Kahlo's childhood home and residence from 1939 until her death in 1954</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG/200px-2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG/300px-2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG/400px-2013-12-22_Grabmal_Frida_Kahlo_Museum_Mexico_City_anagoria.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The garden at La Casa Azul</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Following her separation from Rivera, Kahlo moved back to La Casa Azul and, determined to earn her own living, began another productive period as an artist, inspired by her experiences abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002280–294Zamora199064Kettenmann200352_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002280–294Zamora199064Kettenmann200352-222">[220]</a></sup> Encouraged by the recognition she was gaining, she moved from using the small and more intimate tin sheets she had used since 1932 to large canvases, as they were easier to exhibit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200362Herrera2002315_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200362Herrera2002315-223">[221]</a></sup> She also adopted a more sophisticated technique, limited the graphic details, and began to produce more quarter-length portraits, which were easier to sell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002315_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002315-224">[222]</a></sup> She painted several of her most famous pieces during this period, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Fridas" title="The Two Fridas">The Two Fridas</a></i> (1939), <i>Self-portrait with Cropped Hair</i> (1940), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wounded_Table" title="The Wounded Table">The Wounded Table</a></i> (1940), and <i><a href="/wiki/Self-Portrait_with_Thorn_Necklace_and_Hummingbird" title="Self-Portrait with Thorn Necklace and Hummingbird">Self-Portrait with Thorn Necklace and Hummingbird</a></i> (1940). Three exhibitions featured her works in 1940: the fourth International Surrealist Exhibition in Mexico City, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_International_Exposition" title="Golden Gate International Exposition">Golden Gate International Exposition</a> in San Francisco, and <i>Twenty Centuries of Mexican Art</i> in MoMA in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990136–137Burrus2005220_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990136–137Burrus2005220-225">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 August 1940, Trotsky was assassinated in Coyoacán, where he had continued to live after leaving La Casa Azul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315-227">[225]</a></sup> Kahlo was briefly suspected of being involved, as she knew the murderer, and was arrested and held for two days with her sister Cristina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Burrus2005220_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Burrus2005220-228">[226]</a></sup> The following month, Kahlo traveled to San Francisco for medical treatment for back pain and a fungal infection on her hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002276–277,_295–315Kettenmann200352,_56Zamora199064,_70Burrus2005205_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002276–277,_295–315Kettenmann200352,_56Zamora199064,_70Burrus2005205-229">[227]</a></sup> Her continuously fragile health had increasingly declined since her divorce and was exacerbated by her heavy consumption of alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002276–277,_295–315Kettenmann200352,_56Zamora199064Burrus2005205_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002276–277,_295–315Kettenmann200352,_56Zamora199064Burrus2005205-230">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rivera was also in San Francisco after he fled Mexico City following Trotsky's murder and accepted a commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315_for_fleeingZamora199070Kettenmann200356_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315_for_fleeingZamora199070Kettenmann200356-231">[229]</a></sup> Although Kahlo had a relationship with art dealer <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Berggruen" title="Heinz Berggruen">Heinz Berggruen</a> during her visit to San Francisco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarnham296Ankori2002193_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarnham296Ankori2002193-232">[230]</a></sup> she and Rivera were reconciled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200356–57Burrus2005205Zamora199070_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200356–57Burrus2005205Zamora199070-233">[231]</a></sup> They remarried in a simple civil ceremony on 8 December 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200356–57Burrus2005205Zamora199070Herrera2002295–315_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200356–57Burrus2005205Zamora199070Herrera2002295–315-234">[232]</a></sup> Kahlo and Rivera returned to Mexico soon after their wedding. The union was less turbulent than before for its first five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199086_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199086-235">[233]</a></sup> Both were more independent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Zamora199070_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Zamora199070-236">[234]</a></sup> and while La Casa Azul was their primary residence, Rivera retained the San Ángel house for use as his studio and second apartment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200357_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200357-237">[235]</a></sup> Both continued having extramarital affairs; Kahlo, being <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexual</a>, had affairs with both men and women, with evidence suggesting her male lovers were more important to Kahlo than her female lovers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Zamora199070_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Zamora199070-236">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Despite the medical treatment she had received in San Francisco, Kahlo's health problems continued throughout the 1940s. Due to her spinal problems, she wore twenty-eight separate supportive corsets, varying from steel and leather to plaster, between 1940 and 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–346_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–346-239">[237]</a></sup> She experienced pain in her legs, the infection on her hand had become chronic, and she was also treated for <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–346Zamora199086_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–346Zamora199086-240">[238]</a></sup> The death of her father in April 1941 plunged her into a depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199086_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199086-235">[233]</a></sup> Her ill health made her increasingly confined to La Casa Azul, which became the center of her world. She enjoyed taking care of the house and its garden, and was kept company by friends, servants, and various pets, including <a href="/wiki/Spider_monkey" title="Spider monkey">spider monkeys</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_hairless_dog" title="Mexican hairless dog">Xoloitzcuintlis</a>, and parrots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Zamora199073–78Kettenmann200361_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002295–315Zamora199073–78Kettenmann200361-241">[239]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="593" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png/220px-Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png/330px-Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png/440px-Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frida_Kahlo_in_Sari.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kahlo (centre), <a href="/wiki/Nayantara_Sahgal" title="Nayantara Sahgal">Nayantara Sahgal</a> (right) and Rita Dar at Casa Azul in 1947</div></div></div><p>While Kahlo was gaining recognition in her home country, her health continued to decline. By the mid-1940s, her back had worsened to the point that she could no longer sit or stand continuously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002383Burrus2005205_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002383Burrus2005205-242">[240]</a></sup> In June 1945, she traveled to New York for an operation which fused a bone graft and a steel support to her spine to straighten it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359Burrus2005205_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359Burrus2005205-243">[241]</a></sup> The difficult operation was a failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359-70">[70]</a></sup> According to Herrera, Kahlo also sabotaged her recovery by not resting as required and by once physically re-opening her wounds in a fit of anger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359-70">[70]</a></sup> Her paintings from this period, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Broken_Column" title="The Broken Column">The Broken Column</a></i> (1944), <i>Without Hope</i> (1945), <i>Tree of Hope, Stand Fast</i> (1946), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wounded_Deer" title="The Wounded Deer">The Wounded Deer</a></i> (1946), reflect her declining health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002344–359-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span id="1950.E2.80.931954:_Last_years_and_death"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1950–1954:_Last_years_and_death">1950–1954: Last years and death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4128" data-file-width="2322" decoding="async" height="391" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg/220px-Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg/330px-Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg/440px-Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Studio_di_Frida_Kahlo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kahlo's wheelchair and adjustable easel in La Casa Azul, with one of her still lifes from her final years</div></div></div>
<p>In 1950, Kahlo spent most of the year in Hospital ABC in Mexico City, where she underwent a new bone graft surgery on her spine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990122Herrera2002383–389Burrus2005205_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990122Herrera2002383–389Burrus2005205-244">[242]</a></sup> It caused a difficult infection and necessitated several follow-up surgeries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002389–400_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002389–400-71">[71]</a></sup> After being discharged, she was mostly confined to La Casa Azul, using a wheelchair and crutches to be ambulatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002389–400_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200379Herrera2002389–400-71">[71]</a></sup> During these final years of her life, Kahlo dedicated her time to political causes to the extent that her health allowed. She had rejoined the Mexican Communist Party in 1948<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200380_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKettenmann200380-73">[73]</a></sup> and campaigned for peace, for example, by collecting signatures for the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Appeal" title="Stockholm Appeal">Stockholm Appeal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005206_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005206-245">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Kahlo's right leg was amputated at the knee due to gangrene in August 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430-80">[80]</a></sup> She became severely depressed and anxious, and her dependency on painkillers escalated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430-80">[80]</a></sup> When Rivera began yet another affair, she attempted suicide by overdose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002412–430-80">[80]</a></sup> She wrote in her diary in February 1954, "They amputated my leg six months ago, they have given me centuries of torture and at moments I almost lost my reason. I keep on wanting to kill myself. Diego is what keeps me from it, through my vain idea that he would miss me. ... But never in my life have I suffered more. I will wait a while..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora1990130_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora1990130-246">[244]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4752" data-file-width="3168" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG/220px-2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG/330px-2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG/440px-2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2013-12-22_Frida_Kahlo_Totenmaske_anagoria.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kahlo's <a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">death mask</a> on her bed in La Casa Azul</div></div></div>
<p>In her last days, Kahlo was mostly bedridden with <a href="/wiki/Bronchopneumonia" title="Bronchopneumonia">bronchopneumonia</a>, though she made a public appearance on 2 July 1954, participating with Rivera in a demonstration against the <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat#Operation_PBSUCCESS" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d'état">CIA invasion of Guatemala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433Zamora1990138_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433Zamora1990138-247">[245]</a></sup> She seemed to anticipate her death, as she spoke about it to visitors and drew skeletons and angels in her diary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433-248">[246]</a></sup> The last drawing was a black angel, which biographer <a href="/wiki/Hayden_Herrera" title="Hayden Herrera">Hayden Herrera</a> interprets as the Angel of Death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433-248">[246]</a></sup> It was accompanied by the last words she wrote, "I joyfully await the exit – and I hope never to return – Frida" ("Espero Alegre la Salida – y Espero no Volver jamás").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433_248-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433-248">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>The demonstration worsened her illness, and on the night of 12 July 1954, Kahlo had a high fever and was in extreme pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433_248-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433-248">[246]</a></sup> At approximately 6 a.m. on 13 July 1954, her nurse found her dead in her bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera425–433Zamora199012_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera425–433Zamora199012-249">[247]</a></sup> Kahlo was 47 years old. The official cause of death was <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a>, although no autopsy was performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433_248-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433-248">[246]</a></sup> Herrera has argued that Kahlo, in fact, committed suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerreraOxfordArt_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-HerreraOxfordArt-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433_248-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002425–433-248">[246]</a></sup> The nurse, who counted Kahlo's painkillers to monitor her drug use, stated that Kahlo had taken an overdose the night she died. She had been prescribed a maximum dose of seven pills but had taken eleven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199012_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199012-250">[248]</a></sup> She had also given Rivera a wedding anniversary present that evening, over a month in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZamora199012_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamora199012-250">[248]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the evening of 13 July, Kahlo's body was taken to the <a href="/wiki/Palacio_de_Bellas_Artes" title="Palacio de Bellas Artes">Palacio de Bellas Artes</a>, where it lay in  state under a Communist flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440-251">[249]</a></sup> The following day, it was carried to the Panteón Civil de Dolores, where friends and family attended an informal funeral ceremony. Hundreds of admirers stood outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440-251">[249]</a></sup> In accordance with her wishes, Kahlo was cremated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440_251-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440-251">[249]</a></sup> Rivera, who stated that her death was "the most tragic day of my life", died three years later, in 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440_251-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440-251">[249]</a></sup> Kahlo's ashes are displayed in a pre-Columbian urn at La Casa Azul, which opened as a museum in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440_251-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrera2002433–440-251">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Posthumous_recognition_and_.22Fridamania.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Posthumous_recognition_and_"Fridamania"'>Posthumous recognition and "Fridamania"</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ; background-color: #CCDDFF;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>"The twenty-first-century Frida is both a star – a commercial property complete with fan clubs and merchandising – and an embodiment of the hopes and aspirations of a near-religious group of followers. This wild, hybrid Frida, a mixture of tragic bohemian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_of_Guadalupe" title="Virgin of Guadalupe">Virgin of Guadalupe</a>, revolutionary heroine and <a href="/wiki/Salma_Hayek" title="Salma Hayek">Salma Hayek</a>, has taken such great hold on the public imagination that it tends to obscure the historically retrievable Kahlo."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaddeley200547_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaddeley200547-252">[250]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""> – Art historian <a href="/wiki/Oriana_Baddeley" title="Oriana Baddeley">Oriana Baddeley</a> on Kahlo</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tate_Modern" title="Tate Modern">Tate Modern</a> considers Kahlo "one of the most significant artists of the twentieth century",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> while according to art historian Elizabeth Bakewell, she is "one of Mexico's most important twentieth-century figures".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakewell2001315_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakewell2001315-254">[252]</a></sup> Kahlo's reputation as an artist developed late in her life and grew even further posthumously, as during her lifetime she was primarily known as the wife of Diego Rivera and as an eccentric personality among the international cultural elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori20023–5Deffebach2006189Pankl_and_Blake20122Baddeley199110_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori20023–5Deffebach2006189Pankl_and_Blake20122Baddeley199110-255">[253]</a></sup> She gradually gained more recognition in the late 1970s when feminist scholars began to question the exclusion of female and non-Western artists from the art historical canon and the <a href="/wiki/Chicano_Movement" title="Chicano Movement">Chicano Movement</a> lifted her as one of their icons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori20023–5Deffebach2006189Pankl_and_Blake20121_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori20023–5Deffebach2006189Pankl_and_Blake20121-256">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> The first two books about Kahlo were published in Mexico by <a href="/wiki/Teresa_del_Conde" title="Teresa del Conde">Teresa del Conde</a> and Raquel Tibol in 1976 and 1977, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori20024–5_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori20024–5-258">[256]</a></sup> and, in 1977, <i>The Tree of Hope Stands Firm</i> (1944) became the first Kahlo painting to be sold in an auction, netting $19,000 at <a href="/wiki/Sotheby%27s" title="Sotheby's">Sotheby's</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheran6_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheran6-259">[257]</a></sup> These milestones were followed by the first two retrospectives staged on Kahlo's <i>oeuvre</i> in 1978, one at the Palacio de Bellas Artes in Mexico City and another at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Contemporary_Art,_Chicago" title="Museum of Contemporary Art, Chicago">Museum of Contemporary Art</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkori20024–5_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkori20024–5-258">[256]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two events were instrumental in raising interest in her life and art for the general public outside Mexico. The first was a joint retrospective of her paintings and Tina Modotti's photographs at the <a href="/wiki/Whitechapel_Gallery" title="Whitechapel Gallery">Whitechapel Gallery</a> in London, which was curated and organized by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Wollen" title="Peter Wollen">Peter Wollen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laura_Mulvey" title="Laura Mulvey">Laura Mulvey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWollen2004235–236Pankl_and_Blake20121Baddeley199110_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWollen2004235–236Pankl_and_Blake20121Baddeley199110-260">[258]</a></sup> It opened in May 1982, and later traveled to Sweden, Germany, the United States, and Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWollen2004236_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWollen2004236-261">[259]</a></sup> The second was the publication of art historian Hayden Herrera's international bestseller <i><a href="/wiki/Frida:_A_Biography_of_Frida_Kahlo" title="Frida: A Biography of Frida Kahlo">Frida: A Biography of Frida Kahlo</a></i> in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPankl_and_Blake20121Wollen2004236Baddeley199110_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPankl_and_Blake20121Wollen2004236Baddeley199110-262">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1984, Kahlo's reputation as an artist had grown to such extent that Mexico declared her works part of the national cultural heritage, prohibiting their export from the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheran6_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheran6-259">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artnet_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-artnet-264">[262]</a></sup> As a result, her paintings seldom appear in international auctions, and comprehensive retrospectives are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artnet_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-artnet-264">[262]</a></sup> Regardless, her paintings have still broken records for Latin American art in the 1990s and 2000s. In 1990, she became the first Latin American artist to break the one-million-dollar threshold when <i>Diego and I</i> was auctioned by Sotheby's for $1,430,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheran6_259-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheran6-259">[257]</a></sup> In 2006, <i>Roots</i> (1943) reached US$5.6 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> and in 2016, <i>Two Lovers in a Forest</i> (1939) sold for $8 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kahlo has attracted popular interest to the extent that the term "Fridamania" has been coined to describe the phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPankl_and_Blake20121Baddeley199110–11Lindauer20042_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPankl_and_Blake20121Baddeley199110–11Lindauer20042-267">[265]</a></sup> She is considered "one of the most instantly recognizable artists",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWollen2004236_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWollen2004236-261">[259]</a></sup> whose face has been "used with the same regularity, and often with a shared symbolism, as images of <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bob_Marley" title="Bob Marley">Bob Marley</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaddeley200549_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaddeley200549-268">[266]</a></sup> Her life and art have inspired a variety of merchandise, and her distinctive look has been appropriated by the fashion world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPankl_and_Blake20121Baddeley199110–11Lindauer20042_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPankl_and_Blake20121Baddeley199110–11Lindauer20042-267">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mencimer_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-mencimer-270">[268]</a></sup> A Hollywood biopic, <a href="/wiki/Julie_Taymor" title="Julie Taymor">Julie Taymor</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frida" title="Frida">Frida</a></i>, was released in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> Based on Herrera's biography and starring <a href="/wiki/Salma_Hayek" title="Salma Hayek">Salma Hayek</a> (who co-produced the film) as Kahlo, it grossed US$56 million worldwide and earned six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> nominations, winning for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Makeup" title="Academy Award for Best Makeup">Best Makeup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Academy Award for Best Original Score">Best Original Score</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frida_2002_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frida_2002-272">[270]</a></sup> The 2017 <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Disney</a>-<a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar</a> animation <i><a href="/wiki/Coco_(2017_film)" title="Coco (2017 film)">Coco</a></i> also features Kahlo in a supporting role, voiced by <a href="/wiki/Natalia_Cordova-Buckley" title="Natalia Cordova-Buckley">Natalia Cordova-Buckley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg/220px-Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg/330px-Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg/440px-Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coyoac%C3%A1n_d%C3%ADa_de_muertos_08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Effigy of Kahlo for <a href="/wiki/Day_of_the_Dead" title="Day of the Dead">Day of the Dead</a> at the Museo Frida Kahlo</div></div></div>
<p>Kahlo's popular appeal is seen to stem first and foremost from a fascination with her life story, especially its painful and tragic aspects. She has become an icon for several minority groups and political movements, such as feminists, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LGBTQ" title="LGBTQ">LGBTQ</a> community, and Chicanos. <a href="/wiki/Oriana_Baddeley" title="Oriana Baddeley">Oriana Baddeley</a> has written that Kahlo has become a signifier of non-conformity and "the archetype of a cultural minority", who is regarded simultaneously as "a victim, crippled and abused" and as "a survivor who fights back".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaddeley200547–49_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaddeley200547–49-274">[272]</a></sup> Edward Sullivan stated that Kahlo is hailed as a hero by so many because she is "someone to validate their own struggle to find their own voice and their own public personalities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindauer20041–2_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindauer20041–2-275">[273]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/John_Berger" title="John Berger">John Berger</a>, Kahlo's popularity is partly due to the fact that "the sharing of pain is one of the essential preconditions for a refinding of dignity and hope" in twenty-first century society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerger2001155–165_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerger2001155–165-276">[274]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kirk_Varnedoe" title="Kirk Varnedoe">Kirk Varnedoe</a>, the former chief curator of MoMA, has stated that Kahlo's posthumous success is linked to the way in which "she clicks with today's sensibilities – her psycho-obsessive concern with herself, her creation of a personal alternative world carries a voltage. Her constant remaking of her identity, her construction of a theater of the self are exactly what preoccupy such contemporary artists as <a href="/wiki/Cindy_Sherman" title="Cindy Sherman">Cindy Sherman</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kiki_Smith" title="Kiki Smith">Kiki Smith</a> and, on a more popular level, <a href="/wiki/Madonna" title="Madonna">Madonna</a>... She fits well with the odd, androgynous hormonal chemistry of our particular epoch."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-152">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Kahlo's posthumous popularity and the commercialization of her image have drawn criticism from many scholars and cultural commenters, who think that, not only have many facets of her life been mythologized, but the dramatic aspects of her biography have also overshadowed her art, producing a simplistic reading of her works in which they are reduced to literal descriptions of events in her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindauer20043–12Dexter200511Ankori200531Baddeley200547–53_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindauer20043–12Dexter200511Ankori200531Baddeley200547–53-277">[275]</a></sup> According to journalist Stephanie Mencimer, Kahlo "has been embraced as a poster child for every possible politically correct cause" and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>like a game of telephone, the more Kahlo's story has been told, the more it has been distorted, omitting uncomfortable details that show her to be a far more complex and flawed figure than the movies and cookbooks suggest. This elevation of the artist over the art diminishes the public understanding of Kahlo's place in history and overshadows the deeper and more disturbing truths in her work. Even more troubling, though, is that by airbrushing her biography, Kahlo's promoters have set her up for the inevitable fall so typical of women artists, that time when the contrarians will band together and take sport in shooting down her inflated image, and with it, her art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mencimer_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-mencimer-270">[268]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Baddeley has compared the interest in Kahlo's life to the interest in the troubled life of <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a> but has also stated that a crucial difference between the two is that most people associate Van Gogh with his paintings, whereas Kahlo is usually signified by an image of herself – an intriguing commentary on the way male and female artists are regarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaddeley199111_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaddeley199111-278">[276]</a></sup> Similarly, Peter Wollen has compared Kahlo's cult-like following to that of <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Plath" title="Sylvia Plath">Sylvia Plath</a>, whose "unusually complex and contradictory art" has been overshadowed by simplified focus on her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWollen2004240_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWollen2004240-279">[277]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemorations_and_characterizations">Commemorations and characterizations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2213" data-file-width="1590" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG/170px-Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG/255px-Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG/340px-Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museo_Frida_Kahlo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo_Museum" title="Frida Kahlo Museum">La Casa Azul</a>, which has been open to the public since 1958 as a museum dedicated to Frida Kahlo.</div></div></div>
<p>Kahlo's legacy has been commemorated in several ways. <a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo_Museum" title="Frida Kahlo Museum">La Casa Azul</a>, her home in Coyoacán, was opened as a museum in 1958, and has become one of the most popular museums in Mexico City, with approximately 25,000 visitors monthly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup> The city dedicated a park, Parque Frida Kahlo, to her in Coyoacán in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfgate_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfgate-281">[279]</a></sup> The park features a bronze statue of Kahlo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfgate_281-1"><a href="#cite_note-sfgate-281">[279]</a></sup> In the United States, she became the first Hispanic woman to be honored with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._postage_stamp" title="U.S. postage stamp">U.S. postage stamp</a> in 2001,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> and was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Legacy_Walk" title="Legacy Walk">Legacy Walk</a>, an outdoor public display in Chicago that celebrates <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> history and people, in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kahlo received several commemorations on the centenary of her birth in 2007, and some on the centenary of the birthyear she attested to, 2010. These included the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Mexico" title="Bank of Mexico">Bank of Mexico</a> releasing a new <a href="/wiki/Mexican_peso" title="Mexican peso">MXN$</a> 500-peso note, featuring Kahlo's painting titled <i>Love's Embrace of the Universe, Earth, (Mexico), I, Diego, and Mr. Xólotl</i> (1949) on the reverse of the note and Diego Rivera on the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banxico_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banxico-284">[282]</a></sup> The largest retrospective of her works at Mexico City's Palacio de Bellas Artes attracted approximately 75,000 visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to other tributes, Kahlo's life and art have inspired artists in various fields. In 1984, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Leduc_(film_director)" title="Paul Leduc (film director)">Paul Leduc</a> released a biopic titled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frida,_naturaleza_viva" title="Frida, naturaleza viva">Frida, naturaleza viva</a>,</i> starring <a href="/wiki/Ofelia_Medina" title="Ofelia Medina">Ofelia Medina</a> as Kahlo. She is the protagonist of three fictional novels, Barbara Mujica's <i>Frida</i> (2001),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavenka_Drakulic" title="Slavenka Drakulic">Slavenka Drakulic</a>'s <i>Frida's Bed</i> (2008), and <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Kingsolver" title="Barbara Kingsolver">Barbara Kingsolver</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lacuna" title="The Lacuna">The Lacuna</a></i> (2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup> In 1994, American jazz flautist and composer <a href="/wiki/James_Newton" title="James Newton">James Newton</a> released an album titled <i>Suite for Frida Kahlo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup> Scottish singer/songwriter, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Marra" title="Michael Marra">Michael Marra</a>, wrote a song in homage to Kahlo entitled <i>Frida Kahlo's Visit to the Taybridge Bar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup> In 2017, author Monica Brown and illustrator <a href="/wiki/John_Parra_(illustrator)" title="John Parra (illustrator)">John Parra</a> published a children's book on Kahlo, <i>Frida Kahlo and her Animalitos</i>, which focuses primarily on the animals and pets in Kahlo's life and art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup> In the visual arts, Kahlo's influence has reached wide and far: In 1996, and again in 2005, the Mexican Cultural Institute in Washington, DC coordinated an "Homage to Frida Kahlo" exhibition which showcased Kahlo-related artwork by artists from all over the world in Washington's <a href="/wiki/Fraser_Gallery" title="Fraser Gallery">Fraser Gallery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[290]</a></sup> Additionally, notable artists such as <a href="/wiki/Marina_Abramovi%C4%87" title="Marina Abramović">Marina Abramovic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup> Alana Archer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup> Gabriela Gonzalez Dellosso,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yasumasa_Morimura" title="Yasumasa Morimura">Yasumasa Morimura</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[294]</a></sup> Cris Melo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[295]</a></sup> Rupert Garcia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup> and others have used or appropriated Kahlo's imagery into their own works.
</p><p>Kahlo has also been the subject of several stage performances. <a href="/wiki/Annabelle_Lopez_Ochoa" title="Annabelle Lopez Ochoa">Annabelle Lopez Ochoa</a> choreographed a one-act ballet titled <i><a href="/wiki/Broken_Wings_(ballet)" title="Broken Wings (ballet)">Broken Wings</a></i> for the <a href="/wiki/English_National_Ballet" title="English National Ballet">English National Ballet</a>, which debuted in 2016, <a href="/wiki/Tamara_Rojo" title="Tamara Rojo">Tamara Rojo</a> originated Kahlo in the ballet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dutch_National_Ballet" title="Dutch National Ballet">Dutch National Ballet</a> then commissioned Lopez Ochoa to created a full-length version of the ballet, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frida_(ballet)" title="Frida (ballet)">Frida</a></i>, which premiered in 2020, with <a href="/wiki/Maia_Makhateli" title="Maia Makhateli">Maia Makhateli</a> as Kahlo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup> She also inspired two operas, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Xavier_Rodriguez" title="Robert Xavier Rodriguez">Robert Xavier Rodriguez</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frida_(opera)" title="Frida (opera)">Frida</a></i>, which premiered at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Music_Theater_Festival" title="American Music Theater Festival">American Music Theater Festival</a> in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> in 1991,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[299]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kalevi_Aho" title="Kalevi Aho">Kalevi Aho</a>'s <i>Frida y Diego</i>, which premiered at the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Music_Centre" title="Helsinki Music Centre">Helsinki Music Centre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, Finland in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup> She was the main character in several plays, including Dolores C. Sendler's <i>Goodbye, My Friduchita</i> (1999),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lepage" title="Robert Lepage">Robert Lepage</a> and Sophie Faucher's <i>La Casa Azul</i> (2002),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[302]</a></sup> Humberto Robles' <i>Frida Kahlo: Viva la vida!</i> (2009),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup> and Rita Ortez Provost's <i>Tree of Hope</i> (2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[304]</a></sup> In 2018, <a href="/wiki/Mattel" title="Mattel">Mattel</a> unveiled seventeen new <a href="/wiki/Barbie" title="Barbie">Barbie</a> dolls in celebration of <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women's Day">International Women's Day</a>, including one of Kahlo. Critics objected to the doll's slim waist and noticeably missing <a href="/wiki/Unibrow" title="Unibrow">unibrow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[305]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014 Kahlo was one of the inaugural honorees in the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Honor_Walk" title="Rainbow Honor Walk">Rainbow Honor Walk</a>, a <a href="/wiki/List_of_halls_and_walks_of_fame" title="List of halls and walks of fame">walk of fame</a> in San Francisco's <a href="/wiki/Castro_District,_San_Francisco" title="Castro District, San Francisco">Castro neighborhood</a> noting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LGBTQ" title="LGBTQ">LGBTQ</a> people who have "made significant contributions in their fields".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:022_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-:022-308">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[307]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-310">[308]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, San Francisco Board of Supervisors unanimously voted to rename Phelan Avenue to Frida Kahlo Way. Frida Kahlo Way is the home of City College of San Francisco and Archbishop Riordan High School.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[309]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, Frida was featured on a mural painted by <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Blanco_(artist)" title="Rafael Blanco (artist)">Rafael Blanco</a> in downtown Reno, Nevada.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Solo_exhibitions">Solo exhibitions</span></h2>
<ul><li>8 February – 12 May 2019 – <i>Frida Kahlo: Appearances Can Be Deceiving</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a>. This was the largest U.S. exhibition in a decade devoted solely to the painter and the only U.S. show to feature her Tehuana clothing, hand-painted corsets and other never-before-seen items that had been locked away after the artist's death and rediscovered in 2004.</li>
<li>16 June – 18 November 2018 – <i>Frida Kahlo: Making Her Self Up</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Albert_Museum" title="Victoria and Albert Museum">Victoria and Albert Museum</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[310]</a></sup> The basis for the later Brooklyn Museum exhibit.</li>
<li>3 February – 30 April 2016 – <i>Frida Kahlo: Paintings and Graphic Art From Mexican Collections</i> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faberge_Museum" title="Faberge Museum">Faberge Museum</a>, St. Petersburg. Russia's first retrospective of Kahlo's work.</li>
<li>27 October 2007 – 20 January 2008 – Frida Kahlo an exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Walker_Art_Center" title="Walker Art Center">Walker Art Center</a>, Minneapolis, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a>, 20 February – 18 May 2008; and the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="San Francisco Museum of Modern Art">San Francisco Museum of Modern Art</a>, 16 June – 28 September 2008.</li>
<li>1–15 November 1938 – Frida's <a class="external text" href="https://www.moma.org/explore/inside_out/2009/12/03/a-close-look-frida-kahlo-s-fulang-chang-and-i/#more-185" rel="nofollow">first solo exhibit</a> and New York debut at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>. Georgia O'Keeffe, Isamu Noguchi, and other prominent American artists attended the opening; approximately half of the paintings were sold.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<p><b>Informational notes</b>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kahlo was given her first two names so that she could be baptized according to Catholic traditions, but was always called Frida. She preferred to spell her name "Frieda" until the late 1930s, when she dropped the 'e' as she did not wish to be associated with Germany during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitler" title="Hitler">Hitler</a>'s rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005202Herrera200210–11_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrus2005202Herrera200210–11-133">[133]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Given Kahlo's later problems with <a href="/wiki/Scoliosis" title="Scoliosis">scoliosis</a> and with her hips and limbs, neurologist Budrys Valmantas has argued that she had a congenital condition, <a href="/wiki/Spina_bifida" title="Spina bifida">spina bifida</a>, which was diagnosed by Dr. <a href="/wiki/Leo_Eloesser" title="Leo Eloesser">Leo Eloesser</a> when she was a young adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBudrys20064–10_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBudrys20064–10-151">[150]</a></sup> Psychologist and art historian Dr. Salomon Grimberg disagrees, stating that Kahlo's problems were instead the result of not wearing an orthopedic shoe on her affected right leg, which led to damage to her hips and spine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-152">[151]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEPanzer200440–41,_mentions_1931_letter_from_Kahlo_to_Muray,_but_not_entirely_sure_if_this_was_the_beginning_of_affairMarnham1998234–235,_interprets_letter_as_evidence_of_the_beginning_of_affair-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPanzer200440–41,_mentions_1931_letter_from_Kahlo_to_Muray,_but_not_entirely_sure_if_this_was_the_beginning_of_affairMarnham1998234–235,_interprets_letter_as_evidence_of_the_beginning_of_affair_192-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPanzer2004">Panzer 2004</a>, pp. 40–41, mentions 1931 letter from Kahlo to Muray, but not entirely sure if this was the beginning of affair; <a href="#CITEREFMarnham1998">Marnham 1998</a>, pp. 234–235, interprets letter as evidence of the beginning of affair.</span>
